   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Ocean thermal energy conversion
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy&amp;redirect=no" title="Ocean thermal energy">
       Ocean thermal energy
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:334px;max-width:334px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:332px;max-width:332px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temperaturunterschiede_Ozeane.png">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="291" data-file-width="400" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Temperaturunterschiede_Ozeane.png/330px-Temperaturunterschiede_Ozeane.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Temperaturunterschiede_Ozeane.png 1.5x" width="330"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Worldmap highlighting oceanic regions with high temperature gradients (between surface and 1000m depth)
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:332px;max-width:332px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Otec_produkty-2_(English).png">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="303" data-file-width="400" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Otec_produkty-2_%28English%29.png/330px-Otec_produkty-2_%28English%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/37/Otec_produkty-2_%28English%29.png 1.5x" width="330"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         OTEC diagram and applications
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Ocean thermal energy conversion
      </b>
      (
      <b>
       OTEC
      </b>
      ) uses the temperature difference between cooler deep and warmer shallow or surface
      <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">
       seawaters
      </a>
      to run a
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_engine" title="Heat engine">
       heat engine
      </a>
      and produce useful work, usually in the form of electricity. OTEC is a
      <a href="/wiki/Base_load" title="Base load">
       base load
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">
       electricity generation
      </a>
      system.
     </p>
     <p>
      Among ocean energy sources, OTEC is one of the continuously available
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_resource" title="Renewable energy resource">
       renewable energy resources
      </a>
      that could contribute to base-load power supply.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-:0-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The resource potential for OTEC is considered to be much larger than for other ocean energy forms [World Energy Council, 2000]. Up to 88,000
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TWh" title="TWh">
       TWh
      </a>
      /yr of power could be generated from OTEC without affecting the ocean’s thermal structure [Pelc and Fujita, 2002].
     </p>
     <p>
      Systems may be either closed-cycle or open-cycle. Closed-cycle OTEC uses working fluids that are typically thought of as
      <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">
       refrigerants
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
       ammonia
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/1,1,1,2-Tetrafluoroethane" title="1,1,1,2-Tetrafluoroethane">
       R-134a
      </a>
      . These fluids have low boiling points, and are therefore suitable for powering the system’s generator to generate electricity. The most commonly used heat cycle for OTEC to date is the
      <a href="/wiki/Rankine_cycle" title="Rankine cycle">
       Rankine cycle
      </a>
      , using a low-pressure turbine. Open-cycle engines use vapour from the
      <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">
       seawater
      </a>
      itself as the working fluid.
     </p>
     <p>
      OTEC can also supply quantities of cold water as a by-product. This can be used for air conditioning and refrigeration and the nutrient-rich deep ocean water can feed biological technologies. Another by-product is
      <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">
       fresh water
      </a>
      distilled from the sea.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopSci95_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PopSci95-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      OTEC theory was first developed in the 1880s and the first bench size demonstration model was constructed in 1926. Currently the world's only operating OTEC plant is in Japan, overseen by
      <a href="/wiki/Saga_University" title="Saga University">
       Saga University
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

         <span class="toctext">
          Sources
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48">
        <a href="#External_links">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          18
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          External links
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Attempts to develop and refine OTEC technology started in the 1880s. In 1881,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacques_Arsene_d%27Arsonval" title="Jacques Arsene d'Arsonval">
       Jacques Arsene d'Arsonval
      </a>
      , a French
      <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
       physicist
      </a>
      , proposed tapping the thermal energy of the ocean. D'Arsonval's student,
      <a href="/wiki/Georges_Claude" title="Georges Claude">
       Georges Claude
      </a>
      , built the first OTEC plant, in Matanzas,
      <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">
       Cuba
      </a>
      in 1930.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The system generated 22
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt">
       kW
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">
       electricity
      </a>
      with a low-
      <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">
       pressure
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">
       turbine
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dow-book_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dow-book-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The plant was later destroyed in a storm.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avery.2C_William_H_1994_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avery.2C_William_H_1994-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1935, Claude constructed a plant aboard a 10,000-
      <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">
       ton
      </a>
      cargo vessel moored off the coast of Brazil. Weather and waves destroyed it before it could generate net power.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dow-book_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dow-book-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (Net power is the amount of power generated after subtracting power needed to run the system).
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1956, French scientists designed a 3
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">
       MW
      </a>
      plant for
      <a href="/wiki/Abidjan" title="Abidjan">
       Abidjan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">
       Ivory Coast
      </a>
      . The plant was never completed, because new finds of large amounts of cheap petroleum made it uneconomical.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dow-book_5-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-dow-book-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1962, J. Hilbert Anderson and James H. Anderson, Jr. focused on increasing component efficiency. They patented their new "closed cycle" design in 1967.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-closed-cycle-patent_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-closed-cycle-patent-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This design improved upon the original closed-cycle Rankine system, and included this in an outline for a plant that would produce power at lower cost than oil or coal. At the time, however, their research garnered little attention since coal and nuclear were considered the future of energy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avery.2C_William_H_1994_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avery.2C_William_H_1994-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Japan is a major contributor to the development of OTEC technology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osti-assessment_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-osti-assessment-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Beginning in 1970 the
      <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Electric_Power_Company" title="Tokyo Electric Power Company">
       Tokyo Electric Power Company
      </a>
      successfully built and deployed a 100 kW closed-cycle OTEC plant on the island of
      <a href="/wiki/Nauru" title="Nauru">
       Nauru
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osti-assessment_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-osti-assessment-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The plant became operational on 14 October 1981, producing about 120 kW of electricity; 90 kW was used to power the plant and the remaining electricity was used to power a school and other places.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dow-book_5-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-dow-book-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This set a world record for power output from an OTEC system where the power was sent to a real (as opposed to an experimental) power grid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nauru-outline_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nauru-outline-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      1981 also saw a major development in OTEC technology when Russian engineer, Dr. Alexander Kalina, used a mixture of ammonia and water to produce electricity. This new ammonia-water mixture greatly improved the efficiency of the power cycle. In 1994 Saga University designed and constructed a 4.5 kW plant for the purpose of testing a newly invented Uehara cycle, also named after its inventor Haruo Uehara. This cycle included absorption and extraction processes that allow this system to outperform the Kalina cycle by 1-2%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Currently, the Institute of Ocean Energy, Saga University, is the leader in OTEC power plant research and also focuses on many of the technology's secondary benefits.
     </p>
     <p>
      The 1970s saw an uptick in OTEC research and development during the post 1973 Arab-Israeli War, which caused oil prices to triple. The U.S. federal government poured $260 million into OTEC research after President Carter signed a law that committed the US to a production goal of 10,000 MW of electricity from OTEC systems by 1999.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OTEC_in_Hawaii.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="797" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OTEC_in_Hawaii.jpg/260px-OTEC_in_Hawaii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OTEC_in_Hawaii.jpg/390px-OTEC_in_Hawaii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OTEC_in_Hawaii.jpg/520px-OTEC_in_Hawaii.jpg 2x" width="260"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OTEC_in_Hawaii.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        View of a land based OTEC facility at
        <a href="/wiki/Keahole_Point" title="Keahole Point">
         Keahole Point
        </a>
        on the
        <a href="/wiki/Kona_District,_Hawaii" title="Kona District, Hawaii">
         Kona
        </a>
        coast of
        <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">
         Hawaii
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1974, The U.S. established the
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_Energy_Laboratory_of_Hawaii_Authority" title="Natural Energy Laboratory of Hawaii Authority">
       Natural Energy Laboratory of Hawaii Authority
      </a>
      (NELHA) at
      <a href="/wiki/Keahole_Point" title="Keahole Point">
       Keahole Point
      </a>
      on the
      <a href="/wiki/Kona_District,_Hawaii" title="Kona District, Hawaii">
       Kona coast
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_(island)" title="Hawaii (island)">
       Hawaii
      </a>
      . Hawaii is the best US OTEC location, due to its warm surface water, access to very deep, very cold water, and high electricity costs. The laboratory has become a leading test facility for OTEC technology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elec-costs_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-elec-costs-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the same year, Lockheed received a grant from the U.S. National Science Foundation to study OTEC. This eventually led to an effort by Lockheed, the US Navy, Makai Ocean Engineering, Dillingham Construction, and other firms to build the world's first and only net-power producing OTEC plant, dubbed "Mini-OTEC"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lockheed1_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lockheed1-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For three months in 1979, a small amount of electricity was generated.
     </p>
     <p>
      Research related to making open-cycle OTEC a reality began earnestly in 1979 at the Solar Energy Research Institute (SERI) with funding from the US Department of Energy. Evaporators and suitably configured direct-contact condensers were developed and patented by SERI (see
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ). An original design for a power-producing experiment, then called the 165-kW experiment was described by Kreith and Bharathan (,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ) as the Max Jacob Memorial Award Lecture. The initial design used two parallel axial turbines, using last stage rotors taken from large steam turbines. Later, a team led by Dr. Bharathan at the National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL) developed the initial conceptual design for up-dated 210 kW open-cycle OTEC experiment (
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ). This design integrated all components of the cycle, namely, the evaporator, condenser and the turbine into one single vacuum vessel, with the turbine mounted on top to prevent any potential for water to reach it. The vessel was made of concrete as the first process vacuum vessel of its kind. Attempts to make all components using low-cost plastic material could not be fully achieved, as some conservatism was required for the turbine and the vacuum pumps developed as the first of their kind. Later Dr. Bharathan worked with a team of engineers at the Pacific Institute for High Technology Research (PICHTR) to further pursue this design through preliminary and final stages. It was renamed the Net Power Producing Experiment (NPPE) and was constructed at the Natural Energy Laboratory of Hawaii (NELH) by PICHTR by a team led by Chief Engineer Don Evans and the project was managed by Dr. Luis Vega.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Otec2.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="311" data-file-width="850" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Otec2.jpg/260px-Otec2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Otec2.jpg/390px-Otec2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Otec2.jpg/520px-Otec2.jpg 2x" width="260"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Otec2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
         India
        </a>
        – pipes used for OTEC (left) and floating OTEC plant constructed in 2000 (right)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 2002, India tested a 1 MW floating OTEC pilot plant near Tamil Nadu. The plant was ultimately unsuccessful due to a failure of the deep sea cold water pipe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India" title="Government of India">
       Its government
      </a>
      continues to sponsor research.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2006, Makai Ocean Engineering was awarded a contract from the U.S.
      <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Naval_Research" title="Office of Naval Research">
       Office of Naval Research
      </a>
      (ONR) to investigate the potential for OTEC to produce nationally-significant quantities of hydrogen in at-sea floating plants located in warm, tropical waters. Realizing the need for larger partners to actually commercialize OTEC, Makai approached Lockheed Martin to renew their previous relationship and determine if the time was ready for OTEC. And so in 2007, Lockheed Martin resumed work in OTEC and became a subcontractor to Makai to support their SBIR, which was followed by other subsequent collaborations
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lockheed1_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lockheed1-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In March 2011, Ocean Thermal Energy Corporation signed an Energy Services Agreement (ESA) with the Baha Mar resort, Nassau, Bahamas, for the world's first and largest seawater air conditioning (SWAC) system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In June 2015, the project was put on pause while the resort resolved financial and ownership issues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In August 2016, it was announced that the issues had been resolved and that the resort would open in March 2017.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is expected that the SWAC system's construction will resume at that time.
     </p>
     <p>
      In July 2011, Makai Ocean Engineering completed the design and construction of an OTEC Heat Exchanger Test Facility at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_Energy_Laboratory_of_Hawaii" title="Natural Energy Laboratory of Hawaii">
       Natural Energy Laboratory of Hawaii
      </a>
      . The purpose of the facility is to arrive at an optimal design for OTEC heat exchangers, increasing performance and useful life while reducing cost (heat exchangers being the #1 cost driver for an OTEC plant).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      And in March 2013, Makai announced an award to install and operate a 100 kilowatt turbine on the OTEC Heat Exchanger Test Facility, and once again connect OTEC power to the grid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In July 2016, the Virgin Islands Public Services Commission approved Ocean Thermal Energy Corporation's application to become a Qualified Facility. The Company is thus permitted to begin negotiations with the Virgin Islands Water and Power Authority (WAPA) for a Power Purchase Agreement (PPA) pertaining to an Ocean Thermal Energy Conversion (OTEC) plant on the island of St. Croix. This would be the world's first commercial OTEC plant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Currently_operating_OTEC_plants">
       Currently operating OTEC plants
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Currently operating OTEC plants">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In March 2013, Saga University with various Japanese industries completed the installation of a new OTEC plant. Okinawa Prefecture announced the start of the OTEC operation testing at Kume Island on April 15, 2013. The main aim is to prove the validity of computer models and demonstrate OTEC to the public. The testing and research will be conducted with the support of Saga University until the end of FY 2016. IHI Plant Construction Co. Ltd, Yokogawa Electric Corporation, and Xenesys Inc were entrusted with constructing the 100 kilowatt class plant within the grounds of the Okinawa Prefecture Deep Sea Water Research Center. The location was specifically chosen in order to utilize existing deep seawater and surface seawater intake pipes installed for the research center in 2000. The pipe is used for the intake of deep sea water for research, fishery, and agricultural use.[19] The plant consists of two units; one includes the 50 kW generator while the second unit is used for component testing and optimization.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The OTEC facility and deep seawater research center are open to free public tours by appointment in English and Japanese.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Currently, this is the only fully operational OTEC plant in the world.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2011, Makai Ocean Engineering completed a heat exchanger test facility at NELHA. Used to test a variety of heat exchange technologies for use in OTEC, Makai has received funding to install a 105 kW turbine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Installation will make this facility the largest operational OTEC facility, though the record for largest power will remain with the Open Cycle plant also developed in Hawaii.
     </p>
     <p>
      In July 2014, DCNS group partnered with Akuo Energy announced NER 300 funding for their NEMO project. If successful, the 16MW gross 10MW net offshore plant will be the largest OTEC facility to date. DCNS plans to have NEMO operational by 2020.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An ocean thermal energy conversion power plant built by Makai Ocean Engineering went operational in Hawaii in August 2015 . The governor of Hawaii,
      <a href="/wiki/David_Ige" title="David Ige">
       David Ige
      </a>
      , "flipped the switch" to activate the plant. This is the first true closed-cycle ocean Thermal Energy Conversion (OTEC) plant to be connected to a U.S. electrical grid . It is a demo plant capable of generating 105 kilowatts, enough to power about 120 homes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamic_efficiency">
       Thermodynamic efficiency
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Thermodynamic efficiency">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      A
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_engine" title="Heat engine">
       heat engine
      </a>
      gives greater efficiency when run with a large
      <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
       temperature
      </a>
      difference. In the oceans the temperature difference between surface and deep water is greatest in the
      <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">
       tropics
      </a>
      , although still a modest 20 to 25 °C. It is therefore in the tropics that OTEC offers the greatest possibilities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopSci95_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-PopSci95-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      OTEC has the potential to offer global amounts of energy that are 10 to 100 times greater than other ocean energy options such as
      <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">
       wave power
      </a>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      . OTEC plants can operate continuously providing a
      <a href="/wiki/Base_load" title="Base load">
       base load
      </a>
      supply for an electrical power generation system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopSci95_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-PopSci95-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The main technical challenge of OTEC is to generate significant amounts of power efficiently from small temperature differences. It is still considered an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emerging_technology" title="Emerging technology">
       emerging technology
      </a>
      . Early OTEC systems were 1 to 3 percent
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">
       thermally efficient
      </a>
      , well below the theoretical maximum 6 and 7 percent for this temperature difference.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-countermeasures_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-countermeasures-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Modern designs allow performance approaching the theoretical maximum
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnot_efficiency" title="Carnot efficiency">
       Carnot efficiency
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cycle_types">
       Cycle types
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Cycle types">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Cold seawater is an integral part of each of the three types of OTEC systems: closed-cycle, open-cycle, and hybrid. To operate, the cold seawater must be brought to the surface. The primary approaches are active pumping and desalination. Desalinating seawater near the sea floor lowers its density, which causes it to rise to the surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desalination-pump-patent_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-desalination-pump-patent-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The alternative to costly pipes to bring condensing cold water to the surface is to pump vaporized low boiling point fluid into the depths to be condensed, thus reducing pumping volumes and reducing technical and environmental problems and lowering costs.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Closed">
       Closed
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Closed">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Otec_Closed_Diagram_in_English.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="478" data-file-width="707" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Otec_Closed_Diagram_in_English.JPG/350px-Otec_Closed_Diagram_in_English.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Otec_Closed_Diagram_in_English.JPG/525px-Otec_Closed_Diagram_in_English.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Otec_Closed_Diagram_in_English.JPG/700px-Otec_Closed_Diagram_in_English.JPG 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Otec_Closed_Diagram_in_English.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Diagram of a closed cycle OTEC plant
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Closed-cycle systems use fluid with a low boiling point, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
       ammonia
      </a>
      (having a boiling point around -33 °C at atmospheric pressure), to power a
      <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">
       turbine
      </a>
      to generate electricity. Warm surface
      <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">
       seawater
      </a>
      is pumped through a
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">
       heat exchanger
      </a>
      to vaporize the fluid. The expanding vapor turns the turbo-generator. Cold water, pumped through a second heat exchanger, condenses the vapor into a liquid, which is then recycled through the system.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1979, the Natural Energy Laboratory and several private-sector partners developed the "mini OTEC" experiment, which achieved the first successful at-sea production of net electrical power from closed-cycle OTEC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mini-otec_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mini-otec-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The mini OTEC vessel was moored 1.5 miles (2.4 km) off the Hawaiian coast and produced enough net electricity to illuminate the ship's light bulbs and run its computers and television.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Open">
       Open
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Open">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Otec_Open_Diagram_in_English.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="663" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Otec_Open_Diagram_in_English.JPG/350px-Otec_Open_Diagram_in_English.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Otec_Open_Diagram_in_English.JPG/525px-Otec_Open_Diagram_in_English.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Otec_Open_Diagram_in_English.JPG 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Otec_Open_Diagram_in_English.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Diagram of an open cycle OTEC plant
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Open-cycle OTEC uses warm surface water directly to make electricity. The warm seawater is first pumped into a low-pressure container, which causes it to boil. In some schemes, the expanding
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vapour" title="Vapour">
       vapour
      </a>
      drives a low-pressure turbine attached to an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">
       electrical generator
      </a>
      . The vapour, which has left its
      <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">
       salt
      </a>
      and other contaminants in the low-pressure container, is pure fresh water. It is condensed into a liquid by exposure to cold temperatures from deep-ocean water. This method produces
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desalinization" title="Desalinization">
       desalinized
      </a>
      fresh water, suitable for
      <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">
       drinking water
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">
       irrigation
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">
       aquaculture
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vega05_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vega05-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In other schemes, the rising vapour is used in a
      <a href="/wiki/Gas_lift" title="Gas lift">
       gas lift
      </a>
      technique of lifting water to significant heights. Depending on the embodiment, such
      <a href="/wiki/Mist_lift" title="Mist lift">
       vapour lift
      </a>
      pump techniques generate power from a
      <a href="/wiki/Water_turbine" title="Water turbine">
       hydroelectric turbine
      </a>
      either before or after the pump is used.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jsee_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jsee-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1984, the
      <i>
       Solar Energy Research Institute
      </i>
      (now known as the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">
       National Renewable Energy Laboratory
      </a>
      ) developed a vertical-spout evaporator to convert warm seawater into low-pressure steam for open-cycle plants. Conversion efficiencies were as high as 97% for seawater-to-steam conversion (overall steam production would only be a few percent of the incoming water). In May 1993, an open-cycle OTEC plant at Keahole Point, Hawaii, produced close to 80
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KW" title="KW">
       kW
      </a>
      of electricity during a net power-producing experiment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrel-otec-achievements_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nrel-otec-achievements-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This broke the record of 40 kW set by a Japanese system in 1982.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrel-otec-achievements_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nrel-otec-achievements-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid">
       Hybrid
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hybrid">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A hybrid cycle combines the features of the closed- and open-cycle systems. In a hybrid, warm seawater enters a vacuum chamber and is flash-evaporated, similar to the open-cycle evaporation process. The steam vaporizes the
      <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
       ammonia
      </a>
      working fluid of a closed-cycle loop on the other side of an ammonia vaporizer. The vaporized fluid then drives a turbine to produce electricity. The steam condenses within the heat exchanger and provides
      <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">
       desalinated water
      </a>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/Heat_pipe" title="Heat pipe">
       heat pipe
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Working_fluids">
       Working fluids
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Working fluids">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A popular choice of working fluid is ammonia, which has superior transport properties, easy availability, and low cost. Ammonia, however, is toxic and flammable. Fluorinated carbons such as
      <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">
       CFCs
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HCFC" title="HCFC">
       HCFCs
      </a>
      are not toxic or flammable, but they contribute to ozone layer depletion.
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">
       Hydrocarbons
      </a>
      too are good candidates, but they are highly flammable; in addition, this would create competition for use of them directly as fuels. The power plant size is dependent upon the vapor pressure of the working fluid. With increasing vapor pressure, the size of the turbine and heat exchangers decreases while the wall thickness of the pipe and heat exchangers increase to endure high pressure especially on the evaporator side.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Land.2C_shelf_and_floating_sites">
       Land, shelf and floating sites
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Land, shelf and floating sites">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      OTEC has the potential to produce gigawatts of electrical power, and in conjunction with
      <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">
       electrolysis
      </a>
      , could produce enough hydrogen to completely replace all projected global fossil fuel consumption.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Reducing costs remains an unsolved challenge, however. OTEC plants require a long, large diameter intake pipe, which is submerged a kilometer or more into the ocean's depths, to bring cold water to the surface.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Land-based">
       Land-based
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Land-based">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Land-based and near-shore facilities offer three main advantages over those located in deep water. Plants constructed on or near land do not require sophisticated mooring, lengthy power cables, or the more extensive maintenance associated with open-ocean environments. They can be installed in sheltered areas so that they are relatively safe from storms and heavy seas. Electricity, desalinated water, and cold, nutrient-rich seawater could be transmitted from near-shore facilities via trestle bridges or causeways. In addition, land-based or near-shore sites allow plants to operate with related industries such as
      <a href="/wiki/Mariculture" title="Mariculture">
       mariculture
      </a>
      or those that require desalinated water.
     </p>
     <p>
      Favored locations include those with narrow shelves (volcanic islands), steep (15-20 degrees) offshore slopes, and relatively smooth sea floors. These sites minimize the length of the intake pipe. A land-based plant could be built well inland from the shore, offering more protection from storms, or on the beach, where the pipes would be shorter. In either case, easy access for construction and operation helps lower costs.
     </p>
     <p>
      Land-based or near-shore sites can also support mariculture or chilled water agriculture. Tanks or lagoons built on shore allow workers to monitor and control miniature marine environments. Mariculture products can be delivered to market via standard transport.
     </p>
     <p>
      One disadvantage of land-based facilities arises from the turbulent wave action in the
      <a href="/wiki/Surf_zone" title="Surf zone">
       surf zone
      </a>
      . OTEC discharge pipes should be placed in protective trenches to prevent subjecting them to extreme stress during storms and prolonged periods of heavy seas. Also, the mixed discharge of cold and warm seawater may need to be carried several hundred meters offshore to reach the proper depth before it is released, requiring additional expense in construction and maintenance.
     </p>
     <p>
      One way that OTEC systems can avoid some of the problems and expenses of operating in a surf zone is by building them just offshore in waters ranging from 10 to 30 meters deep (Ocean Thermal Corporation 1984). This type of plant would use shorter (and therefore less costly) intake and discharge pipes, which would avoid the dangers of turbulent surf. The plant itself, however, would require protection from the marine environment, such as breakwaters and erosion-resistant foundations, and the plant output would need to be transmitted to shore.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_Based_Design_and_Location_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Land_Based_Design_and_Location-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Shelf_based">
       Shelf based
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Shelf based">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      To avoid the turbulent surf zone as well as to move closer to the cold-water resource, OTEC plants can be mounted to the continental shelf at depths up to 100 meters (330 ft). A shelf-mounted plant could be towed to the site and affixed to the sea bottom. This type of construction is already used for offshore oil rigs. The complexities of operating an OTEC plant in deeper water may make them more expensive than land-based approaches. Problems include the stress of open-ocean conditions and more difficult product delivery. Addressing strong ocean currents and large waves adds engineering and construction expense. Platforms require extensive pilings to maintain a stable base. Power delivery can require long underwater cables to reach land. For these reasons, shelf-mounted plants are less attractive.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_Based_Design_and_Location_41-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Land_Based_Design_and_Location-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Floating">
       Floating
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Floating">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Floating OTEC facilities operate off-shore. Although potentially optimal for large systems, floating facilities present several difficulties. The difficulty of mooring plants in very deep water complicates power delivery. Cables attached to floating platforms are more susceptible to damage, especially during storms. Cables at depths greater than 1000 meters are difficult to maintain and repair. Riser cables, which connect the sea bed and the plant, need to be constructed to resist entanglement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_Based_Design_and_Location_41-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Land_Based_Design_and_Location-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As with shelf-mounted plants, floating plants need a stable base for continuous operation. Major storms and heavy seas can break the vertically suspended cold-water pipe and interrupt warm water intake as well. To help prevent these problems, pipes can be made of flexible polyethylene attached to the bottom of the platform and gimballed with joints or collars. Pipes may need to be uncoupled from the plant to prevent storm damage. As an alternative to a warm-water pipe, surface water can be drawn directly into the platform; however, it is necessary to prevent the intake flow from being damaged or interrupted during violent motions caused by heavy seas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_Based_Design_and_Location_41-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Land_Based_Design_and_Location-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Connecting a floating plant to power delivery cables requires the plant to remain relatively stationary. Mooring is an acceptable method, but current mooring technology is limited to depths of about 2,000 meters (6,600 ft). Even at shallower depths, the cost of mooring may be prohibitive.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Some_proposed_projects">
       Some proposed projects
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Some proposed projects">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      OTEC projects under consideration include a small plant for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" title="U.S. Navy">
       U.S. Navy
      </a>
      base on the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
       British
      </a>
      overseas territory island of
      <a href="/wiki/Diego_Garcia" title="Diego Garcia">
       Diego Garcia
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">
       Indian Ocean
      </a>
      . Ocean Thermal Energy Corporation (formerly OCEES International, Inc.) is working with the U.S. Navy on a design for a proposed 13-MW OTEC plant, to replace the current diesel generators. The OTEC plant would also provide 1.25 million gallons
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2012)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      per day of potable water. This project is currently waiting for changes in US military contract policies. OTE has proposed building a 10-MW OTEC plant on
      <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">
       Guam
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bahamas">
       Bahamas
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bahamas">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.otecorporation.com" rel="nofollow">
       Ocean Thermal Energy Corporation (OTE)
      </a>
      currently has plans to install two 10 MW OTEC plants in the US Virgin Islands and a 5-10 MW OTEC facility in The Bahamas. OTE has also designed the world’s largest
      <a class="external text" href="http://otecorporation.com/technology/swac-101-how-does-it-work/" rel="nofollow">
       Seawater Air Conditioning (SWAC)
      </a>
      plant for a resort in The Bahamas, which will use cold deep seawater as a method of air-conditioning.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In mid-2015, the 95%-complete project was temporarily put on hold while the resort resolved financial and ownership issues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In August 22, 2016, the government of the Bahamas announced that a new agreement had been signed under which the Baha Mar resort will be completed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On September 27, 2016, Bahamian Prime Minister Perry Christie announced that construction had resumed on Baha Mar, and that the resort was slated to open in March 2017.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      OTE expects to have the SWAC plant up and running within two years of Baha Mar's opening.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hawaii">
       Hawaii
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hawaii">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">
       Lockheed Martin
      </a>
      's Alternative Energy Development team has partnered with Makai Ocean Engineering
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      to complete the final design phase of a 10-MW closed cycle OTEC pilot system which planned to become operational in
      <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">
       Hawaii
      </a>
      in the 2012-2013 time frame. This system was designed to expand to 100-MW commercial systems in the near future. In November, 2010 the U.S.
      <a href="/wiki/Naval_Facilities_Engineering_Command" title="Naval Facilities Engineering Command">
       Naval Facilities Engineering Command
      </a>
      (NAVFAC) awarded Lockheed Martin a US$4.4 million contract modification to develop critical system components and designs for the plant, adding to the 2009 $8.1 million contract and two
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">
       Department of Energy
      </a>
      grants totaling over $1 million in 2008 and March 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A small but operational ocean thermal energy conversion (OTEC) plant was inaugurated in Hawaii in August 2015. The opening of the research and development 100-kilowatt facility marked the first time a closed-cycle OTEC plant was connected to the U.S. grid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hainan">
       Hainan
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hainan">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      On April 13, 2013 Lockheed contracted with the Reignwood Group to build a 10 megawatt plant off the coast of southern China to provide power for a planned resort on
      <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">
       Hainan
      </a>
      island.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E.26E5113_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-E.26E5113-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A plant of that size would power several thousand homes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters41613_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reuters41613-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LMNews_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LMNews-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Reignwood Group acquired Opus Offshore in 2011 which forms its Reignwood Ocean Engineering division which also is engaged in development of
      <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_drilling" title="Deepwater drilling">
       deepwater drilling
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Opus_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Opus-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">
       Japan
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Japan">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Currently the only continuously operating OTEC system is located in Okinawa Prefecture, Japan. The Governmental support, local community support, and advanced research carried out by Saga University were key for the contractors, IHI Plant Construction Co. Ltd, Yokogawa Electric Corporation, and Xenesys Inc, to succeed with this project. Work is being conducted to develop a 1MW facility on Kume Island requiring new pipelines. In July 2014, more than 50 members formed the Global Ocean reSource and Energy Association (
      <a class="external text" href="http://gosea.info/en/" rel="nofollow">
       GOSEA
      </a>
      ) an international organization formed to promote the development of the Kumejima Model and work towards the installation of larger deep seawater pipelines and a 1MW OTEC Facility.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The companies involved in the current OTEC projects, along with other interested parties have developed plans for offshore OTEC systems as well.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      - For more details, see "Currently Operating OTEC Plants" above.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_Virgin_Islands">
       United States Virgin Islands
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: United States Virgin Islands">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      On March 5, 2014, Ocean Thermal Energy Corporation (OTEC)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the 30th Legislature of the United States Virgin Islands (USVI) signed a Memorandum of Understanding to move forward with a study to evaluate the feasibility and potential benefits to the USVI of installing on-shore Ocean Thermal Energy Conversion (OTEC) renewable energy power plants and Seawater Air Conditioning (SWAC) facilities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The benefits to be assessed in the USVI study include both the baseload (24/7) clean electricity generated by OTEC, as well as the various related products associated with OTEC and SWAC, including abundant fresh drinking water, energy-saving air conditioning, sustainable aquaculture and mariculture, and agricultural enhancement projects for the Islands of St Thomas and St Croix. The Honorable Shawn-Michael Malone, President of the USVI Senate, commented on his signing of the Memorandum of Understanding (MOU) authorizing OTE's feasibility study.
      <i>
       “The most fundamental duty of government is to protect the health and welfare of its citizens,"
      </i>
      said Senator Malone.
      <i>
       "These clean energy technologies have the potential to improve the air quality and environment for our residents, and to provide the foundation for meaningful economic development. Therefore, it is our duty as elected representatives to explore the feasibility and possible benefits of OTEC and SWAC for the people of USVI.”
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On July 18, 2016, OTE's application to be a Qualifying Facility was approved by the Virgin Islands Public Services Commission.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      OTE also received permission to begin negotiating contracts associated with this project.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Kiribati">
       Kiribati
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Kiribati">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      South Korea's Research Institute of Ships and Ocean Engineering (KRISO) received Approval in Principal from Bureau Veritas for their 1MW offshore OTEC design. No timeline was given for the project which will be located 6 km offshore of the Republic of Kiribati.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Martinique">
       Martinique
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Martinique">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Akuo Energy and DCNS were awarded NER300 funding on July 8, 2014
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      for their NEMO (New Energy for Martinique and Overseas) project which is expected to be a 10.7MW-net offshore facility completed in 2020.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The award to help with development totaled 72 million Euro.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Related_activities">
       Related activities
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Related activities">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      OTEC has uses other than power production.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Desalination">
       Desalination
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Desalination">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Desalinated water can be produced in open- or hybrid-cycle plants using
      <a href="/wiki/Surface_condenser" title="Surface condenser">
       surface condensers
      </a>
      to turn evaporated seawater into potable water. System analysis indicates that a 2-megawatt plant could produce about 4,300 cubic metres (150,000 cu ft) of desalinated water each day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another system patented by Richard Bailey creates condensate water by regulating deep ocean water flow through surface condensers correlating with fluctuating dew-point temperatures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This condensation system uses no incremental energy and has no moving parts.
     </p>
     <p>
      On March 22, 2015, Saga University opened a Flash-type desalination demonstration facility on Kumejima.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saga_University_Report_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Saga_University_Report-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This satellite of their Institute of Ocean Energy uses post-OTEC deep seawater from the Okinawa OTEC Demonstration Facility and raw surface seawater to produce desalinated water. Air is extracted from the closed system with a vacuum pump. When raw sea water is pumped into the flash chamber it boils, allowing pure steam to rise and the salt and remaining seawater to be removed. The steam is returned to liquid in a heat exchanger with cold post-OTEC deep seawater.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOES_Satellite_Kumejima_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IOES_Satellite_Kumejima-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The desalinated water can be used in hydrogen production or drinking water (if minerals are added).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Air_conditioning">
       Air conditioning
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Air conditioning">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The 41 °F (5 °C) cold seawater made available by an OTEC system creates an opportunity to provide large amounts of cooling to industries and homes near the plant. The water can be used in chilled-water coils to provide air-conditioning for buildings. It is estimated that a pipe 1 foot (0.30 m) in diameter can deliver 4,700 gallons of water per minute. Water at 43 °F (6 °C) could provide more than enough air-conditioning for a large building. Operating 8,000 hours per year in lieu of electrical conditioning selling for 5-10¢ per kilowatt-hour, it would save $200,000-$400,000 in energy bills annually.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/InterContinental" title="InterContinental">
       InterContinental
      </a>
      Resort and Thalasso-Spa on the island of
      <a href="/wiki/Bora_Bora" title="Bora Bora">
       Bora Bora
      </a>
      uses an SWAC system to air-condition its buildings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The system passes seawater through a heat exchanger where it cools freshwater in a closed loop system. This freshwater is then pumped to buildings and directly cools the air.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2010, Copenhagen Energy opened a district cooling plant in Copenhagen, Denmark. The plant delivers cold seawater to commercial and industrial buildings, and has reduced electricity consumption by 80 percent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ocean Thermal Energy Corporation (OTE) has designed a 9800-ton SDC system for a vacation resort in The Bahamas.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chilled-soil_agriculture">
       Chilled-soil agriculture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Chilled-soil agriculture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      OTEC technology supports chilled-soil agriculture. When cold seawater flows through underground pipes, it chills the surrounding soil. The temperature difference between roots in the cool soil and leaves in the warm air allows plants that evolved in
      <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">
       temperate climates
      </a>
      to be grown in the
      <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">
       subtropics
      </a>
      . Dr. John P. Craven, Dr. Jack Davidson and Richard Bailey patented this process and demonstrated it at a research facility at the Natural Energy Laboratory of Hawaii Authority (NELHA).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The research facility demonstrated that more than 100 different crops can be grown using this system. Many normally could not survive in Hawaii or at Keahole Point.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Japan has also been researching agricultural uses of Deep Sea Water since 2000 at the Okinawa Deep Sea Water Research Institute on Kume Island. The Kume Island facilities use regular water cooled by Deep Sea Water in a heat exchanger run through pipes in the ground to cool soil. Their techniques have developed an important resource for the island community as they now produce spinach, a winter vegetable, commercially year round. An expansion of the deep seawater agriculture facility was completed by Kumejima Town next to the OTEC Demonstration Facility in 2014. The new facility is for researching the economic practicality of chilled-soil agriculture on a larger scale.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Aquaculture">
       Aquaculture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Aquaculture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">
       Aquaculture
      </a>
      is the best-known byproduct, because it reduces the financial and energy costs of pumping large volumes of water from the deep ocean. Deep ocean water contains high concentrations of essential nutrients that are depleted in surface waters due to biological consumption. This "artificial upwelling" mimics the natural upwellings that are responsible for fertilizing and supporting the world's largest marine ecosystems, and the largest densities of life on the planet.
     </p>
     <p>
      Cold-water delicacies, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">
       salmon
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">
       lobster
      </a>
      , thrive in this nutrient-rich, deep, seawater.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microalgae" title="Microalgae">
       Microalgae
      </a>
      such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spirulina_(dietary_supplement)" title="Spirulina (dietary supplement)">
        Spirulina
       </a>
      </i>
      , a health food supplement, also can be cultivated. Deep-ocean water can be combined with surface water to deliver water at an optimal temperature.
     </p>
     <p>
      Non-native species such as salmon, lobster,
      <a href="/wiki/Abalone" title="Abalone">
       abalone
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Trout" title="Trout">
       trout
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">
       oysters
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">
       clams
      </a>
      can be raised in pools supplied by OTEC-pumped water. This extends the variety of fresh seafood products available for nearby markets. Such low-cost refrigeration can be used to maintain the quality of harvested fish, which deteriorate quickly in warm tropical regions. In Kona, Hawaii, aquaculture companies working with NELHA generate about $40 million annually, a significant portion of Hawaii’s GDP.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The NELHA plant established in 1993 produced an average of 7,000 gallons of freshwater per day. KOYO USA was established in 2002 to capitalize on this new economic opportunity. KOYO bottles the water produced by the NELHA plant in Hawaii. With the capacity to produce one million bottles of water every day, KOYO is now Hawaii’s biggest exporter with $140 million in sales.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_production">
       Hydrogen production
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Hydrogen production">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       Hydrogen
      </a>
      can be produced via
      <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">
       electrolysis
      </a>
      using OTEC electricity. Generated steam with electrolyte compounds added to improve efficiency is a relatively pure medium for hydrogen production. OTEC can be scaled to generate large quantities of hydrogen. The main challenge is cost relative to other energy sources and fuels.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mineral_extraction">
       Mineral extraction
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Mineral extraction">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The ocean contains 57
      <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">
       trace elements
      </a>
      in salts and other forms and dissolved in solution. In the past, most economic analyses concluded that mining the ocean for trace elements would be unprofitable, in part because of the energy required to pump the water. Mining generally targets minerals that occur in high concentrations, and can be extracted easily, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
       magnesium
      </a>
      . With OTEC plants supplying water, the only cost is for extraction.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      The Japanese investigated the possibility of extracting
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">
       uranium
      </a>
      and found developments in other technologies (especially materials sciences) were improving the prospects.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Political_concerns">
       Political concerns
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Political concerns">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Because OTEC facilities are more-or-less stationary surface platforms, their exact location and legal status may be affected by the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">
       United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea
      </a>
