      treaty (UNCLOS). This treaty grants coastal nations 12- and 200-nautical-mile (370 km) zones of varying legal authority from land, creating potential conflicts and regulatory barriers. OTEC plants and similar structures would be considered
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_islands" title="Artificial islands">
       artificial islands
      </a>
      under the treaty, giving them no independent legal status. OTEC plants could be perceived as either a threat or potential partner to
      <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_management" title="Fisheries management">
       fisheries
      </a>
      or to seabed mining operations controlled by the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Seabed_Authority" title="International Seabed Authority">
       International Seabed Authority
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_and_economics">
       Cost and economics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cost and economics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      For OTEC to be viable as a power source, the technology must have tax and subsidy treatment similar to competing energy sources. Because OTEC systems have not yet been widely deployed, cost estimates are uncertain. One study estimates power generation costs as low as US $0.07 per kilowatt-hour, compared with $0.05 - $0.07 for subsidized wind systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Beneficial factors that should be taken into account include OTEC's lack of waste products and fuel consumption, the area in which it is available,
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2008)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      (often within 20° of the equator)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the geopolitical effects of
      <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">
       petroleum
      </a>
      dependence, compatibility with alternate forms of ocean power such as wave energy, tidal energy and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methane_hydrate" title="Methane hydrate">
       methane hydrates
      </a>
      , and supplemental uses for the seawater.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamics">
       Thermodynamics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Thermodynamics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      A rigorous treatment of OTEC reveals that a 20 °C temperature difference will provide as much energy as a hydroelectric plant with 34 m head for the same volume of water flow. The low temperature difference means that water volumes must be very large to extract useful amounts of heat. A 100MW power plant would be expected to pump on the order of 12 million gallons (44,400 metric tonnes) per minute.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For comparison, pumps must move a mass of water greater than the weight of the
      <a href="/wiki/German_battleship_Bismarck" title="German battleship Bismarck">
       Battleship Bismark
      </a>
      , which weighed 41,700 metric tons, every minute. This makes pumping a substantial
      <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_loss" title="Parasitic loss">
       parasitic drain
      </a>
      on energy production in OTEC systems, with one Lockheed design consuming 19.55 MW in pumping costs for every 49.8 MW net electricity generated. For OTEC schemes using heat exchangers, to handle this volume of water the exchangers need to be enormous compared to those used in conventional thermal power generation plants,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Da_Rosa_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Da_Rosa-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      making them one of the most critical components due to their impact on overall efficiency. A 100 MW OTEC power plant would require 200 exchangers each larger than a 20-foot shipping container making them the single most expensive component.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Variation_of_ocean_temperature_with_depth">
       Variation of ocean temperature with depth
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Variation of ocean temperature with depth">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The total
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">
       insolation
      </a>
      received by the oceans (covering 70% of the earth's surface, with clearness index of 0.5 and average energy retention of 15%) is:
      <span class="texhtml">
       5.45×10
       <sup>
        18
       </sup>
       MJ/yr × 0.7 × 0.5 × 0.15 = 2.87×10
       <sup>
        17
       </sup>
       MJ/yr
      </span>
     </p>
     <p>
      We can use
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambert%27s_law" title="Lambert's law">
       Lambert's law
      </a>
      to quantify the solar energy absorption by water,
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mfrac>
               <mrow>
                <mi>
                 d
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 I
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 (
                </mo>
                <mi>
                 y
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 )
                </mo>
               </mrow>
               <mrow>
                <mi>
                 d
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 y
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mfrac>
             </mrow>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mi>
              μ
              <!-- μ -->
             </mi>
             <mi>
              I
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle -{\frac {dI(y)}{dy}}=\mu I}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="-\frac{dI(y)}{dy}=\mu I" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ae3f518127df653afed5c3d2bda10b220710ade5" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:13.763ex; height:6.343ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      where,
      <i>
       y
      </i>
      is the depth of water,
      <i>
       I
      </i>
      is intensity and
      <i>
       μ
      </i>
      is the absorption coefficient. Solving the above
      <a href="/wiki/Differential_equation" title="Differential equation">
       differential equation
      </a>
      ,
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              I
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mi>
              y
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               I
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                0
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mi>
              exp
             </mi>
             <mo>
              ⁡
              <!-- ⁡ -->
             </mo>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <mi>
              μ
              <!-- μ -->
             </mi>
             <mi>
              y
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo>
             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle I(y)=I_{0}\exp(-\mu y)\,}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt=" I(y)=I_{0}\exp(-\mu y) \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fd1922497985b705fdcc1da3e7011ffd32136f4a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:19.967ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The absorption coefficient
      <i>
       μ
      </i>
      may range from 0.05 m
      <sup>
       −1
      </sup>
      for very clear fresh water to 0.5 m
      <sup>
       −1
      </sup>
      for very salty water.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the intensity
      <a href="/wiki/Exponential_decay" title="Exponential decay">
       falls exponentially
      </a>
      with depth
      <i>
       y
      </i>
      , heat absorption is concentrated at the top layers. Typically in the tropics, surface temperature values are in excess of 25 °C (77 °F), while at 1 kilometer (0.62 mi), the temperature is about 5–10 °C (41–50 °F). The warmer (and hence lighter) waters at the surface means there are no
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convection_current" title="Convection current">
       thermal convection currents
      </a>
      . Due to the small temperature gradients, heat transfer by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_conduction" title="Heat conduction">
       conduction
      </a>
      is too low to equalize the temperatures. The ocean is thus both a practically infinite heat source and a practically infinite heat sink.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2013)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      This temperature difference varies with latitude and season, with the maximum in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical">
       tropical
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical" title="Subtropical">
       subtropical
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equatorial_climate" title="Equatorial climate">
       equatorial
      </a>
      waters. Hence the tropics are generally the best OTEC locations.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Open.2FClaude_cycle">
       Open/Claude cycle
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Open/Claude cycle">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In this scheme, warm surface water at around 27 °C (81 °F) enters an evaporator at pressure slightly below the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturation_pressure" title="Saturation pressure">
       saturation pressures
      </a>
      causing it to vaporize.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               H
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                1
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               H
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                f
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle H_{1}=H_{f}\,}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt=" H_{1}=H_{f} \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a4410e3682fdf83887afebe7ba6e7fe07e7a10f8" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:9.585ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Where
      <i>
       H
       <sub>
        f
       </sub>
      </i>
      is
      <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">
       enthalpy
      </a>
      of liquid water at the inlet temperature,
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <sub>
       1
      </sub>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Otec_oc_t-s_dia.jpg">
       <img alt="Otec oc t-s dia.jpg" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="482" height="433" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b5/Otec_oc_t-s_dia.jpg" width="482"/>
      </a>
     </p>
     <p>
      This temporarily
      <a href="/wiki/Superheating" title="Superheating">
       superheated
      </a>
      water undergoes volume boiling as opposed to pool boiling in conventional boilers where the heating surface is in contact. Thus the water partially flashes to steam with two-phase equilibrium prevailing. Suppose that the pressure inside the evaporator is maintained at the saturation pressure,
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      .
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

              </mi>

               <mn>
                2
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               H
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                1
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>

              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                f
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              +
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mn>
                2
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               H
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                f
               </mi>
               <mi>
                g
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle H_{2}=H_{1}=H_{f}+x_{2}H_{fg}\,}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="H_{2}=H_{1}=H_{f}+x_{2}H_{fg} \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/910a751feaf62dd817343bc4ab294679e9b86b76" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:24.832ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Here,
      <i>
       x
      </i>
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      is the fraction of water by mass that vaporizes. The warm water mass flow rate per unit
      <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">
       turbine
      </a>
      mass flow rate is 1/
      <i>
       x
      </i>
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The low pressure in the evaporator is maintained by a
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_pump" title="Vacuum pump">
       vacuum pump
      </a>
      that also removes the dissolved non-condensable gases from the evaporator. The evaporator now contains a mixture of water and steam of very low
      <a href="/wiki/Vapor_quality" title="Vapor quality">
       vapor quality
      </a>
      (steam content). The steam is separated from the water as saturated vapor. The remaining water is saturated and is discharged to the ocean in the open cycle. The steam is a low pressure/high
      <a href="/wiki/Specific_volume" title="Specific volume">
       specific volume
      </a>
      working fluid. It expands in a special low pressure turbine.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

              </mi>

               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>

              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>

               </mi>
              </mrow>

             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>

         </math>

      Here,
      <i>

       <sub>

      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <sub>
       2
      </sub>

      <a href="/wiki/Isentropic_process" title="Isentropic process">
       isentropic
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reversible_adiabatic" title="Reversible adiabatic">
       reversible adiabatic
      </a>
      ) turbine,
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

             </mo>

     <p>
      The above equation corresponds to the temperature at the exhaust of the turbine,
      <i>

      The enthalpy at

      This enthalpy is lower. The adiabatic reversible turbine work =

      Actual turbine work
      <span class="texhtml">

       <i>
        polytropic efficiency
       </i>

     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

             </mo>
             <mtext>
             </mtext>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

              </mi>

             <mtext>
             </mtext>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="H_{5}=H_{3}-\ \mathrm{actual}\ \mathrm{work} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e3ac41968a0de92c898a2362e6153609cdeacb0" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:24.413ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The condenser temperature and pressure are lower. Since the turbine exhaust is to be discharged back into the ocean, a direct contact condenser is used to mix the exhaust with cold water, which results in a near-saturated water. That water is now discharged back to the ocean.
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       H
      </i>

      </sub>

      </sub>

     <p>
      The temperature differences between stages include that between warm surface water and working steam, that between exhaust steam and cooling water, and that between cooling water reaching the condenser and deep water. These represent external
      <a href="/wiki/Reversible_process_(thermodynamics)" title="Reversible process (thermodynamics)">
       irreversibilities
      </a>
      that reduce the overall temperature difference.
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Closed_Anderson_cycle">
       Closed Anderson cycle
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Closed Anderson cycle">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      a Developed starting in the 1960s by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=J._Hilbert_Anderson&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="J. Hilbert Anderson (page does not exist)">
       J. Hilbert Anderson
      </a>
      of Sea Solar Power, Inc. In this cycle,
      <i>
       Q
       <sub>
        H
       </sub>
      </i>
      is the heat transferred in the evaporator from the warm sea water to the working fluid. The working fluid exits the evaporator as a gas near its
      <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">
       dew point
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The high-pressure, high-temperature gas then is expanded in the turbine to yield turbine work,
      <i>
       W
       <sub>
        T
       </sub>
      </i>
      . The working fluid is slightly superheated at the turbine exit and the turbine typically has an efficiency of 90% based on reversible, adiabatic expansion.
     </p>
     <p>
      From the turbine exit, the working fluid enters the condenser where it rejects heat,
      <i>
       -Q
       <sub>
        C
       </sub>
      </i>
      , to the cold sea water. The condensate is then compressed to the highest pressure in the cycle, requiring condensate pump work,
      <i>
       W
       <sub>
        C
       </sub>
      </i>
      . Thus, the Anderson closed cycle is a Rankine-type cycle similar to the conventional power plant steam cycle except that in the Anderson cycle the working fluid is never superheated more than a few
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degrees_Fahrenheit" title="Degrees Fahrenheit">
       degrees Fahrenheit
      </a>
      . Owing to viscous effects, working fluid pressure drops in both the evaporator and the condenser. This pressure drop, which depends on the types of heat exchangers used, must be considered in final design calculations but is ignored here to simplify the analysis. Thus, the parasitic condensate pump work,
      <i>
       W
       <sub>
        C
       </sub>
      </i>
      , computed here will be lower than if the heat exchanger pressure drop was included. The major additional parasitic energy requirements in the OTEC plant are the cold water pump work,
      <i>
       W
       <sub>
        CT
       </sub>
      </i>
      , and the warm water pump work,
      <i>
       W
       <sub>
        HT
       </sub>
      </i>
      . Denoting all other parasitic energy requirements by
      <i>
       W
       <sub>
        A
       </sub>
      </i>
      , the net work from the OTEC plant,
      <i>
       W
       <sub>
        NP
       </sub>
      </i>
      is
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                N
               </mi>
               <mi>
                P
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                C
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                C
               </mi>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
               <mi>
                T
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                A
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle W_{NP}=W_{T}-W_{C}-W_{CT}-W_{HT}-W_{A}\,}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt=" W_{NP}=W_{T}-W_{C}-W_{CT}-W_{HT}-W_{A} \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/76fa19549c0b9fe578b4c1de14b171ba4381202e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:40.938ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The thermodynamic cycle undergone by the working fluid can be analyzed without detailed consideration of the parasitic energy requirements. From the first law of thermodynamics, the energy balance for the working fluid as the system is
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                N
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               Q
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               Q
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                C
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle W_{N}=Q_{H}-Q_{C}\,}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt=" W_{N}=Q_{H}-Q_{C} \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a6365f0e0cb747dbff848b0e6e3611943d5fc317" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:17.135ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      where
      <span class="texhtml">
       <i>
        W
        <sub>
         N
        </sub>
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        W
        <sub>
         T
        </sub>
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        W
        <sub>
         C
        </sub>
       </i>
      </span>
      is the net work for the thermodynamic cycle. For the idealized case in which there is no working fluid pressure drop in the heat exchangers,
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               Q
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mo>
               ∫
               <!-- ∫ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <msub>
              <mi>

              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mi>
              d
             </mi>
             <mi>
              s
             </mi>
             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle Q_{H}=\int _{H}T_{H}ds\,}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt=" Q_{H}=\int_{H}T_{H}ds \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3e0b7a4a265f3caf9e6addd3ab7b53bb4f0170db" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:15.827ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      and
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               Q
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                C
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mo>
               ∫
               <!-- ∫ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                C
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               T
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                C
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mi>
              d
             </mi>
             <mi>
              s
             </mi>
             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle Q_{C}=\int _{C}T_{C}ds\,}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt=" Q_{C}=\int_{C}T_{C}ds \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/11f691018f567724fef9cf26c09755633763ae3f" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:15.196ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      so that the net thermodynamic cycle work becomes
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span>
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                N
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mo>
               ∫
               <!-- ∫ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               T
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                H
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mi>
              d
             </mi>
             <mi>
              s
             </mi>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mo>
               ∫
               <!-- ∫ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                C
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <msub>
              <mi>
               T
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                C
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mi>
              d
             </mi>
             <mi>
              s
             </mi>
             <mspace width="thinmathspace">
             </mspace>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle W_{N}=\int _{H}T_{H}ds-\int _{C}T_{C}ds\,}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt=" W_{N}=\int_{H}T_{H}ds-\int_{C}T_{C}ds \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/83894d195c9fc1f0efff93388af0bda359abfd0d" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:27.395ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Subcooled liquid enters the evaporator. Due to the heat exchange with warm sea water, evaporation takes place and usually superheated vapor leaves the evaporator. This vapor drives the turbine and the 2-phase mixture enters the condenser. Usually, the subcooled liquid leaves the condenser and finally, this liquid is pumped to the evaporator completing a cycle.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_Impact">
       Environmental Impact
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Environmental Impact">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Carbon dioxide dissolved in deep cold and high pressure layers is brought up to the surface and released as the water warms.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Mixing of deep ocean water with shallower water brings up nutrients and makes them available to shallow water life. This may be an advantage for aquaculture of commercially important species, but may also unbalance the ecological system around the power plant.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      OTEC plants use very large flows of warm surface seawater and cold deep seawater to generate constant renewable power. The deep seawater is oxygen deficient and generally 20-40 times more nutrient rich (in nitrate and nitrite) than shallow seawater. When these plumes are mixed, they are slightly denser than the ambient seawater.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bioplume_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bioplume-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though no large scale physical environmental testing of OTEC has been done, computer models have been developed to simulate the effect of OTEC plants.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrodynamic_modeling">
       Hydrodynamic modeling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Hydrodynamic modeling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2010, a computer model was developed to simulate the physical oceanographic effects of one or several 100 megawatt OTEC plant(s). The model suggests that OTEC plants can be configured such that the plant can conduct continuous operations, with resulting temperature and nutrient variations that are within naturally occurring levels. Studies to date suggest that by discharging the OTEC flows downwards at a depth below 70 meters, the dilution is adequate and nutrient enrichment is small enough so that 100 megawatt OTEC plants could be operated in a sustainable manner on a continuous basis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydroplume_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hydroplume-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_modeling">
       Biological modeling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Biological modeling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The nutrients from an OTEC discharge could potentially cause increased biological activity if they accumulate in large quantities in the
      <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">
       photic zone
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydroplume_82-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hydroplume-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2011 a biological component was added to the hydrodynamic computer model to simulate the biological response to plumes from 100 megawatt OTEC plants. In all cases modeled (discharge at 70 meters depth or more), no unnatural variations occurs in the upper 40 meters of the ocean's surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bioplume_81-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-bioplume-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The picoplankton response in the 110 - 70 meter depth layer is approximately a 10-25% increase, which is well within naturally occurring variability. The nanoplankton response is negligible. The enhanced productivity of diatoms (microplankton) is small. The subtle phytoplankton increase of the baseline OTEC plant suggests that higher-order biochemical effects will be very small.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bioplume_81-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-bioplume-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Studies">
       Studies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Studies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A previous Final Environmental Impact Statement (EIS) for the United States' NOAA from 1981 is available,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but needs to be brought up to current oceanographic and engineering standards. Studies have been done to propose the best environmental baseline monitoring practices, focusing on a set of ten chemical oceanographic parameters relevant to OTEC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most recently, NOAA held an OTEC Workshop in 2010 and 2012 seeking to assess the physical, chemical, and biological impacts and risks, and identify information gaps or needs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(database)" title="Tethys (database)">
       Tethys database
      </a>
      provides access to scientific literature and general information on the potential environmental effects of OTEC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_difficulties">
       Technical difficulties
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Technical difficulties">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dissolved_gases">
       Dissolved gases
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Dissolved gases">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The performance of direct contact heat exchangers operating at typical OTEC boundary conditions is important to the Claude cycle. Many early Claude cycle designs used a surface condenser since their performance was well understood. However, direct contact condensers offer significant disadvantages. As cold water rises in the intake pipe, the pressure decreases to the point where gas begins to evolve. If a significant amount of gas comes out of solution, placing a gas trap before the direct contact heat exchangers may be justified. Experiments simulating conditions in the warm water intake pipe indicated about 30% of the dissolved gas evolves in the top 8.5 meters (28 ft) of the tube. The trade-off between
      <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dearation" title="wikt:dearation">
       pre-dearation
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      of the seawater and expulsion of non-condensable gases from the condenser is dependent on the gas evolution dynamics, deaerator efficiency, head loss, vent compressor efficiency and parasitic power. Experimental results indicate vertical spout condensers perform some 30% better than falling jet types.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Microbial_fouling">
       Microbial fouling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Microbial fouling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Because raw seawater must pass through the heat exchanger, care must be taken to maintain good
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">
       thermal conductivity
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Biofouling" title="Biofouling">
       Biofouling
      </a>
      layers as thin as 25 to 50 micrometres (0.00098 to 0.00197 in) can degrade heat exchanger performance by as much as 50%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-countermeasures_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-countermeasures-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 1977 study in which mock heat exchangers were exposed to seawater for ten weeks concluded that although the level of microbial fouling was low, the thermal conductivity of the system was significantly impaired.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-fouling_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-fouling-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The apparent discrepancy between the level of fouling and the heat transfer impairment is the result of a thin layer of water trapped by the microbial growth on the surface of the heat exchanger.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-fouling_89-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-fouling-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another study concluded that fouling degrades performance over time, and determined that although regular brushing was able to remove most of the microbial layer, over time a tougher layer formed that could not be removed through simple brushing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-countermeasures_35-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-countermeasures-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The study passed sponge rubber balls through the system. It concluded that although the ball treatment decreased the fouling rate it was not enough to completely halt growth and brushing was occasionally necessary to restore capacity. The microbes regrew more quickly later in the experiment (i.e. brushing became necessary more often) replicating the results of a previous study.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-brush-cleaning_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-brush-cleaning-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The increased growth rate after subsequent cleanings appears to result from selection pressure on the microbial colony.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-brush-cleaning_90-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-brush-cleaning-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Continuous use of 1 hour per day and intermittent periods of free fouling and then
      <a href="/wiki/Water_chlorination" title="Water chlorination">
       chlorination
      </a>
      periods (again 1 hour per day) were studied. Chlorination slowed but did not stop microbial growth; however chlorination levels of .1 mg per liter for 1 hour per day may prove effective for long term operation of a plant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-countermeasures_35-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-countermeasures-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The study concluded that although microbial fouling was an issue for the warm surface water heat exchanger, the cold water heat exchanger suffered little or no biofouling and only minimal inorganic fouling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-countermeasures_35-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-countermeasures-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Besides water temperature, microbial fouling also depends on nutrient levels, with growth occurring faster in nutrient rich water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biofilm-dynamics_91-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-biofilm-dynamics-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The fouling rate also depends on the material used to construct the heat exchanger.
      <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">
       Aluminium
      </a>
      tubing slows the growth of microbial life, although the
      <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">
       oxide
      </a>
      layer which forms on the inside of the pipes complicates cleaning and leads to larger efficiency losses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-brush-cleaning_90-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-brush-cleaning-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In contrast,
      <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
       titanium
      </a>
      tubing allows biofouling to occur faster but cleaning is more effective than with aluminium.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microbial-brush-cleaning_90-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-microbial-brush-cleaning-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sealing">
       Sealing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Sealing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The evaporator, turbine, and condenser operate in partial vacuum ranging from 3% to 1% of atmospheric pressure. The system must be carefully sealed to prevent in-leakage of atmospheric air that can degrade or shut down operation. In closed-cycle OTEC, the specific volume of low-pressure steam is very large compared to that of the pressurized working fluid. Components must have large flow areas to ensure steam velocities do not attain excessively high values.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Parasitic_power_consumption_by_exhaust_compressor">
       Parasitic power consumption by exhaust compressor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Parasitic power consumption by exhaust compressor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      An approach for reducing the exhaust compressor
      <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_loss" title="Parasitic loss">
       parasitic power loss
      </a>
      is as follows. After most of the steam has been condensed by spout condensers, the non-condensible gas steam mixture is passed through a counter current region which increases the gas-steam reaction by a factor of five. The result is an 80% reduction in the exhaust pumping power requirements.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_air.2Fwarm_water_conversion">
       Cold air/warm water conversion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Cold air/warm water conversion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In winter in coastal
      <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">
       Arctic
      </a>
      locations, the delta T between the seawater and ambient air can be as high as 40 °C (72 °F). Closed-cycle systems could exploit the air-water temperature difference. Eliminating seawater extraction pipes might make a system based on this concept less expensive than OTEC. This technology is due to H. Barjot, who suggested butane as cryogen, because of its boiling point of −0.5 °C (31.1 °F) and its non-solubility in water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Assuming a level of efficiency of realistic 4%, calculations show that the amount of energy generated with one cubic meter water at a temperature of 2 °C (36 °F) in a place with an air temperature of −22 °C (−8 °F) equals the amount of energy generated by letting this cubic meter water run through a hydroelectric plant of 4000 feet (1,200 m) height.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Barjot Polar Power Plants could be located on islands in the polar region or designed as swimming barges or platforms attached to the
      <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">
       ice cap
      </a>
      . The weather station Myggbuka at Greenlands east coast for example, which is only 2,100 km away from Glasgow, detects monthly mean temperatures below −15 °C (5 °F) during 6 winter months in the year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Application_of_the_thermoelectric_effect">
       Application of the thermoelectric effect
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Application of the thermoelectric effect">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In 2014 Liping Liu, Associate Professor at Rutgers University, envisioned an OTEC system that utilises the solid state
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoelectric_effect" title="Thermoelectric effect">
       thermoelectric effect
      </a>
      rather than the fluid cycles traditionally used.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
       See also
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
